    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

  <span id="freeText10675292812821395170" style="display:none">What do Katie-Rose, Yasaman, Milla, and Violet all have in common? Other than being named after flowers, practically nothing. Katie-Rose is a film director in training. Yasaman is a computer whiz. Milla is third in command of the A list. And Violet is the new girl in school. They’re fab girls, all of them, but they sure aren’t friends. And if evil queen bee Medusa— ’scuse me, Modessa—has her way, they never will be. But this is the beginning of a new school year, when anything can happen and social worlds can collide . . .<br /> <br />Told in Lauren Myracle’s inventive narrative style—here a fresh mix of instant messages, blog posts, screenplay, and straight narrative—<i>Luv Ya Bunches</i>, the first in a four-book series, is a funny, honest depiction of the shifting alliances and rivalries that shape school days, and of the lasting friendships that blossom from the skirmishes.</span>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I don't think there are any swear words in it... as far as I know, they dirtiest word in the book means a dangling piece of dung. (Which is gross, but<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I don't think there are any swear words in it... as far as I know, they dirtiest word in the book means a dangling piece of dung. (Which is gross, but not that age-inappropriate) It's a pretty easy read. (Which is good, because there are some things she might have questions about- one girl has two moms, another girl is a Muslim- that she might have questions about, and you can read it if needed.)<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

<div id="review_537339790" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/537339790" />
    <a title="Mayra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7888683-mayra"><img alt="Mayra" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/537339790?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mayra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mayra" href="/user/show/7888683-mayra">Mayra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer537339790" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6861928521996770939">This book is so adorable, and I love the cover. I gave it away because I thought I was "too mature" but now I kinda wish I still had it. I love all the characters, and I love how they're all original and yet easy to relate to. Violet is the secretive new girl, Yasaman is the shy outcast, Camilla is the anxious pretty popular girl, and Katie Rose is the bossy film geek. What do they have in common? They're each named after flowers, in some way, shape or form. Neither of them have any friends. It </span>
  <span id="freeText6861928521996770939" style="display:none">This book is so adorable, and I love the cover. I gave it away because I thought I was "too mature" but now I kinda wish I still had it. I love all the characters, and I love how they're all original and yet easy to relate to. Violet is the secretive new girl, Yasaman is the shy outcast, Camilla is the anxious pretty popular girl, and Katie Rose is the bossy film geek. What do they have in common? They're each named after flowers, in some way, shape or form. Neither of them have any friends. It seems like Yasaman was always alienated for being Muslim, and teased by the super-bitchy Modessa/Medusa. Camilla technically has a niche she fits into, but from the start it's clear she's unhappy. I was interested in the idea that whenever her friends do something terrible to her, she breaks off the friendship, but she always comes running back. I felt like I could relate to her more than the other girls. She is the only one who seems to care about her reputation, and she also worries constantly, which makes her more relatable to me. Katie Rose was really cute. She seemed like someone I might want to be friends with. I read the second one, and I think it'd be cool to have a book that shows these four good friends in middle or high school. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6861928521996770939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_69792210" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69792210" />
    <a title="Nely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1440151-nely"><img alt="Nely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1271166357p2/1440151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69792210?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nely" href="/user/show/1440151-nely">Nely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1440151-nely?shelf=for-review">for-review</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69792210" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8820134581809235177">In Luv Ya Bunches, Katie-Rose, Milla, Violet and Yasaman are all experiencing their first week in the 5th grade.<br/><br/>Katie-Rose is the techy one - she normally hides behind her camcorder. She and Camilla or “Milla” for short became friends over the summer at their summer camp. But now that school is about to start Katie-Rose is stressing because she’s not sure if her friendship with Milla will continue.<br/><br/>Milla is the beautiful, popular one. She wears all the trendy clothes, and hangs out with the coole</span>
  <span id="freeText8820134581809235177" style="display:none">In Luv Ya Bunches, Katie-Rose, Milla, Violet and Yasaman are all experiencing their first week in the 5th grade.<br /><br />Katie-Rose is the techy one - she normally hides behind her camcorder. She and Camilla or “Milla” for short became friends over the summer at their summer camp. But now that school is about to start Katie-Rose is stressing because she’s not sure if her friendship with Milla will continue.<br /><br />Milla is the beautiful, popular one. She wears all the trendy clothes, and hangs out with the coolest girls in school (although Modessa and Quin - said coolest girls, are not very nice). She lives with her two mothers and is stressing because slowly she is realizing that the coolest girls in school might not exactly be who she wants to hang out with.<br /><br />Violet is the new girl in school. She just moved with her father to town and is none too happy about having to leave her friends behind. Not to mention she is suffering over the loss of her mother. She is assessed by the other girls and is promptly offered to be part of the popular clique. She wears the right clothes and has the perfect attitude - but will she fit in?<br /><br />Then there’s Yasaman - although she’s been in the same school with these other girls, she has always been seen as an outcast. Her classmates see her as different because she wears a hijab and comes from a Muslim family. But underneath all that she is quite the whiz at html code. She has created a social network (similar to MySpace, Facebook, etc.) but she has no friends to tell about it.<br /><br />The story is told through all four points-of-view, including some fun instant message chats, and some very inventive screenplays and daydreams (courtesy of Katie-Rose). Each girl has her own personality and they all four compliment one another. It touches on topics that are very real to young girls - anywhere from bullying, to fashion sense, to just everyday issues - like overcoming differences and accepting who you are. I loved all the illustrations and the cutesy chat forums were uber adorable. I think all young girls can enjoy this story - it is definitely geared towards the 9-12 age group. It is also noted that this is book one - so we will definitely be reading more about the girls in the future.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8820134581809235177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_78426570" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78426570" />
    <a title="Noam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46173-noam"><img alt="Noam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294699136p2/46173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78426570?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Noam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Noam" href="/user/show/46173-noam">Noam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46173-noam?shelf=anglo-america">anglo-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46173-noam?shelf=juvenalia">juvenalia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46173-noam?shelf=white-author">white-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78426570" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5070953143246987112">Honestly, I picked up the Advance Reader's Copy for my brother as an "ironic" birthday present, because he is "not a "hipster." " I mean, really, an internet-speak book with a pointedly diverse cast - for ten year olds? It seemed a bit much.<br/><br/>But after hearing about the Scholastic controversy, I borrowed it back and read it. It was a LOT better than I was expecting! I think some things were inappropriate for elementary-schoolers (I'm surprised Scholastic cared about Milla's parents' gender but not</span>
  <span id="freeText5070953143246987112" style="display:none">Honestly, I picked up the Advance Reader's Copy for my brother as an "ironic" birthday present, because he is "not a "hipster." " I mean, really, an internet-speak book with a pointedly diverse cast - for ten year olds? It seemed a bit much.<br /><br />But after hearing about the Scholastic controversy, I borrowed it back and read it. It was a LOT better than I was expecting! I think some things were inappropriate for elementary-schoolers (I'm surprised Scholastic cared about Milla's parents' gender but not about references to male anatomy). Yes, the group of friends was perhaps unrealistically diverse, but Myracle actually addresses racism, and not just the blatant bullying, but also the preconceptions that Yasaman's friends have about her because she's Muslim. And while no one says anything blatantly homophobic to Milla about her mothers, the bully uses mean-spirited insinuation to get to her.<br /><br />Overall, good job Myracle. I'm glad someone's out there writing about race and LGBT issues for mainstream culture.<br /><br />* * * * *<br /><br />Edit: I wrote this review about 5 years ago, but I've just started getting unexpected likes on it recently, so I'm revisiting it:<br /><br />I regret that I wrote that the cast is unrealistically diverse. This is based on my own unfortunate experience growing up in an incredibly self-segregated community, and the fact that each girl was from a different ethnic background really did seem very out of the ordinary to me. I now feel that, just like giving one of the characters two mothers, making the cast ethnically diverse, and specifically addressing stereotypes through Yasaman's interactions with her friends, were a really important move. It's a shame how little diversity there is in mainstream literature of any genre, and how most books with significant non-white characters are marginalized as "special interest" books. While <u>Luv Ya Bunches</u> was not necesarily the best literature in the world, let's be honest, neither are <u>Harry Potter</u> or <u>Hunger Games</u> - they're entertainment. At least <u>Luv Ya Bunches</u> is entertainment that acknowledges that not everyone is white, male and hetero, so I'm bumping it up to 3 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5070953143246987112" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_76493029" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/370770-lisa-w?shelf=teen">teen</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer76493029&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76493029" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1979490079814742814">From the Publisher<br/>What do Katie-Rose, Yasaman, Milla, and Violet all have in common? Other than being named after flowers, practically nothing. Katie-Rose is a film director in training. Yasaman is a computer whiz. Milla is third in command of the A list. And Violet is the new girl in school. They’re fab girls, all of them, but they sure aren’t friends. And if evil queen bee Medusa— ’scuse me, Modessa—has her way, they never will be. But this is the beginning of a new school year, when anything </span>
  <span id="freeText1979490079814742814" style="display:none">From the Publisher<br />What do Katie-Rose, Yasaman, Milla, and Violet all have in common? Other than being named after flowers, practically nothing. Katie-Rose is a film director in training. Yasaman is a computer whiz. Milla is third in command of the A list. And Violet is the new girl in school. They’re fab girls, all of them, but they sure aren’t friends. And if evil queen bee Medusa— ’scuse me, Modessa—has her way, they never will be. But this is the beginning of a new school year, when anything can happen and social worlds can collide . . .<br /> Told in Lauren Myracle’s inventive narrative style—here a fresh mix of instant messages, blog posts, screenplay, and straight narrative—Luv Ya Bunches, the first in a four-book series, is a funny, honest depiction of the shifting alliances and rivalries that shape school days, and of the lasting friendships that blossom from the skirmishes. <br /><br />Publishers Weekly<br />Blending instant messaging and screenplay-styled text into the narrative, Myracle (ttyl) begins a new series about the woes of being a tween, featuring four likable heroines all named after flowers. Katie Rose, Camilla (aka Milla), Yasaman and Violet come from different backgrounds and have distinct passions and insecurities (Yasaman is Muslim and an expert with computers; Milla, who has two mothers, struggles with her sense of self). But each could use a new friend as she begins fifth grade (“What Katie-Rose wants is a real friend, the kind that lasts forever”). Their shared distaste for meanness and their enthusiasm for the social networking site that Yasaman creates help draw the girls together—and their desire to get revenge against cruel queen bee Modessa, who has hurt each of them at some point, seals their camaraderie. The novel sends something of a mixed message about the acceptability of teasing as the girls’ plot their own prank in response to Modessa’s machinations. Still, readers will find the girls’ voices enticing and should be able to relate to their conflicts and inner anxieties. Ages 9–13.(Oct.) <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_276641741" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/276641741" />
    <a title="Natalie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4265643-natalie"><img alt="Natalie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585543407p2/4265643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/276641741?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie" href="/user/show/4265643-natalie">Natalie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4265643-natalie?shelf=hate-it">hate-it</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer276641741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5284035339519260650">Oh, Lauren...did you really think you could get away with <i>another</i> tawdry chick-lit? Apparently so. This book's so-called modern, tweeny style is in fact used and crumbling. Any actual tween to read this book will cringe at the fake text-speak and squealing, shallow protagonists. <br/><u>High Points.</u><br/>Again, I'm not sure what to put here. If Myracle had cut down into only one person's story, she could have made this better with some serious editing. I liked Katie-Rose well enough, so if we had focused just</span>
  <span id="freeText5284035339519260650" style="display:none">Oh, Lauren...did you really think you could get away with <i>another</i> tawdry chick-lit? Apparently so. This book's so-called modern, tweeny style is in fact used and crumbling. Any actual tween to read this book will cringe at the fake text-speak and squealing, shallow protagonists. <br /><u>High Points.</u><br />Again, I'm not sure what to put here. If Myracle had cut down into only one person's story, she could have made this better with some serious editing. I liked Katie-Rose well enough, so if we had focused just on her and her life, then the story would've been better, maybe scraping two and a half stars.<br /><u>Low Points.</u><br />Stereotyping. All of the girls in the book fit into some sort of ethnic, religious or just plain fluffy middle-school one. The characters are flat and uninteresting, all of them Mary Sues.<br /><br />The plot is even more fluffy and unoriginal than the characters, what little there is, just some "popularity" kind of theme going on. <br /><br />The aforementioned "text speak." Anyone who misspells their titles should make any sensible reader run at least five miles in the opposite direction. If you're going to go with "ya" at least spell love correctly. I cringe, Laurie, I cringe.<br /><br />The rest of the writing is pretty much awful.<br /><br />I mean, I could go on, but the point is it's just plain <i>bad</i>, no whims about it. STAY AWAY.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5284035339519260650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65773017?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alea" href="/user/show/134374-alea">Alea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/134374-alea?shelf=read-for-review">read-for-review</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65773017" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14098926709051621493">I loved the diversity in Luv Ya Bunches, from the characters ethnic backgrounds, to their home situations, to their friends at school, these four girls realize that none of that matters when it comes to true friendship. I also loved the diversity of storytelling methods such as IM conversations, screenplay format, and regular narrative. While format changes are a personal favorite of mine I think they also work well for reluctant readers.<br/><br/>While I had to keep reminding myself that the girls were 5</span>
  <span id="freeText14098926709051621493" style="display:none">I loved the diversity in Luv Ya Bunches, from the characters ethnic backgrounds, to their home situations, to their friends at school, these four girls realize that none of that matters when it comes to true friendship. I also loved the diversity of storytelling methods such as IM conversations, screenplay format, and regular narrative. While format changes are a personal favorite of mine I think they also work well for reluctant readers.<br /><br />While I had to keep reminding myself that the girls were 5th graders and not in junior high (I guess things were different when I was their age!) I think this story of friendship can be appreciate by anyone from late elementary school age to junior high and beyond, I mean heck I'm nowhere near their ages and I enjoyed reading it!<br /><br />While I liked all of the characters, personally my favorite character was Yasaman. She's a bit of a loner, a computer/internet guru and a Muslim. It's very exciting to see more and more Muslim characters in young adult and childrens books recently. I'm also excited that this is a start of a series and that we will get to know a lot more about this group of new friends!</span>

        <a title="Jessica Mitchell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Mitchell" href="/user/show/12348913-jessica-mitchell">Jessica Mitchell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Little middle school girls who love to 'giggle' or 'squeal'.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jessica by: </span>
      My friend, Tamara
  </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer552149001" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7442040755684128295">Jessica Mitchell, 11<br/>Luv Ya Bunches<br/>4 stars<br/><br/>(Im just gonna stick right to the point) Most of this book was amazing, I never got bored. The girly drama that I've managed to stay away from was quite funny and entertaining. The reason it didnt get five stars was simply because the characters were unrealistic. Very. I mean, I know its a book, but still.<br/><br/>THE REAL WAY THINGS ARE IN 5TH GRADE: (You should trust me, thats the grade im in now.)<br/><br/>-The popular girls arent bossy and smart, They're ghetto, and alw</span>
  <span id="freeText7442040755684128295" style="display:none">Jessica Mitchell, 11<br />Luv Ya Bunches<br />4 stars<br /><br />(Im just gonna stick right to the point) Most of this book was amazing, I never got bored. The girly drama that I've managed to stay away from was quite funny and entertaining. The reason it didnt get five stars was simply because the characters were unrealistic. Very. I mean, I know its a book, but still.<br /><br />THE REAL WAY THINGS ARE IN 5TH GRADE: (You should trust me, thats the grade im in now.)<br /><br />-The popular girls arent bossy and smart, They're ghetto, and always talk about 'dicks' and other things like that.<br /><br />-The girls arent nice like the girls in this book. They dont tease eachother playfully like we do, me and my friends just walk up to eachother and say 'hey faget', than start talking about anime.<br /><br />-If you tell your friends to join a website, they'll say 'okay', than forget about it forever because they're too lazy to make an account.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7442040755684128295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

So one day, a collision happens at school (with all the girls) that causes Milla to lose her precious Tally the turtle. This whole collision hads a lot of friendship and confusion. <br />What i really love about this book is the IMing, the whole situation, their life at home and at school, and the writing itself.<br />Lauren Myracle, my favorite author that i hope i meet one day, always have this way when she writes, the beginning always interesting, and when it's around 100 pages left of the book, it sucks me completely which is hard to put down. I love this book so much, even if i haven't read her new one yet, i still love her to pieces.     <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76287808?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rieh Medin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rieh Medin" href="/user/show/686478-rieh-medin">Rieh Medin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76287808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14547510973720991707">Honestly, I didn't expect much from this book. I have tried reading "TTYL" but could never get that far into it. <br/><br/>This book ended up surprising me. Instead of thinking it kiddish and unimportant, I was thinking of my own childhood and comparing it. Though I didn't have so much of an internet fad going on in my childhood, the rest was almost exactly like I experienced and I found myself getting more and more nostalgic. It might not have been the best of times, but when your biggest worry is if you</span>
  <span id="freeText14547510973720991707" style="display:none">Honestly, I didn't expect much from this book. I have tried reading "TTYL" but could never get that far into it. <br /><br />This book ended up surprising me. Instead of thinking it kiddish and unimportant, I was thinking of my own childhood and comparing it. Though I didn't have so much of an internet fad going on in my childhood, the rest was almost exactly like I experienced and I found myself getting more and more nostalgic. It might not have been the best of times, but when your biggest worry is if your friend likes you or not?<br /><br />The storyline is also engaging. Rather than be entirely in 'chat speak', Myracle mixes it up this time with regular prose, which I found to be just what was needed to keep the story going. <br /><br />In all I'd say this would be an excellent book for anyone from 4-6th grade especially, but older ages would probably enjoy the light-hearted prose and characters. <br /><br />(And when there's a mention of 'penitheth'? It's most likely they've heard about it already. About a hundred times.) </span>
  <a data-text-id="14547510973720991707" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_76053657" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/76053657" />
    <a title="Librariann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/126589-librariann"><img alt="Librariann" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239212940p2/126589.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/76053657?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Librariann" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Librariann" href="/user/show/126589-librariann">Librariann</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126589-librariann?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126589-librariann?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/126589-librariann?shelf=chick-lit">chick-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer76053657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9827013355482681677">Ages 9+<br/><br/>So anyway, after hearing all of the SCHOLASTIC DOESN'T WANT BOOK-FAIR GOING ELEMENTARY SCHOOLERS TO BE EXPOSED TO A SAME SEX COUPLE hubbub, and being appropriately outraged in various social medias (fb, greader, yalsa-bk, etc) I figured the responsible thing to do would be to read the book. <br/><br/>Truth: I didn't think I'd be interested in the book initially, because when I had flipped through the ARC back in July, I was like, ag, chat transcripts, blech. (Sorry Lauren Myracle, but I had to say </span>
  <span id="freeText9827013355482681677" style="display:none">Ages 9+<br /><br />So anyway, after hearing all of the SCHOLASTIC DOESN'T WANT BOOK-FAIR GOING ELEMENTARY SCHOOLERS TO BE EXPOSED TO A SAME SEX COUPLE hubbub, and being appropriately outraged in various social medias (fb, greader, yalsa-bk, etc) I figured the responsible thing to do would be to read the book. <br /><br />Truth: I didn't think I'd be interested in the book initially, because when I had flipped through the ARC back in July, I was like, ag, chat transcripts, blech. (Sorry Lauren Myracle, but I had to say L8r G8r to your ttyl series) The good news is that the chat transcripts, being broken up by prose text as they are, don't bother me. <br /><br />Luv Ya Bunches is a fairly textbook fifth grade friendship drama, with mean girls and new girls and popular girls and geeks, but it's well done. It's funny and happens to include a cross section of kids you might come across today in any fifth grade classroom: Muslim techie girl, geeky filmmaker girl, dark-skinned new girl whose mom is receiving inpatient treatment for bipolarism, and - it just so happens - popular girl who has two moms. I love that her having two moms doesn't mean she's teased or picked on. She's popular! And just happens to have two moms! <br /><br />(Two moms who, BTW do NOT engage in graphic lesbian sex ANYWHERE in the 335 page book. I know you are SHOCKED.) <br /><br />The funny thing is, the same-sex relationship isn't even what I would pick to be the most Angry Mom baiting topic in the book. (An honor that I award to a headgear sporting girl who gives a straight-from-Wikipedia oral presentation about the Greek satyrs and their talent of balancing wine cups on their erect "penitheth.") Runner-up: the definition of "dingleberry." HEY! I didn't know what that was until HIGH SCHOOL!<br /><br />All in all, a read that will appeal to many 4-5 grade girls - and probably a few in third grade too - that is one of the VERY few mainstream titles that takes a refreshing "shrug - it's just the way it is" approach to same-sex parents. Here here, Lauren Myracle. And if I were placing bets, I'd say that most girls today won't even realize there's a hubbub to be had. <br /><br />In conclusion, here's what I said to yalsa-bkers regarding the Scholastic Scandal: "For me, the issue is not so much inclusion in the book fairs (although that is problematic, to be sure), it’s that Scholastic’s editorial process requested the REMOVAL of a character’s same sex parents from the novel. That, to me, reeks of an outdated and biased attitude. <br /><br />Props to Lauren Myracle for standing up for her book as it was written. I would much rather deal with the problem of whether a book with GLBT content should be excluded from a Book Fair (IMHO, it shouldn’t, but that’s another fight) than be unable to find/purchase for my library middle-grade books that reflect a same-sex parent household."<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9827013355482681677" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lara " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6092714-lara"><img alt="Lara " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1427320737p2/6092714.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201295235?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lara " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lara " href="/user/show/6092714-lara">Lara </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6092714-lara?shelf=i-own-a-copy">i-own-a-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201295235" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13944988794989661061">**WARNING: includes spoilers**<br/><br/>Another awesome novel by Lauren Myracle =)<br/> I wouldn't say this is her best, but it's still pretty good. I couldn't relate at all, though-- fifth grade was not like this for me. There were no ice cream socials, no Greek weeks, no Medusas, none of that. <br/>Still, this is a very fun read. Finished it in one sitting. Lauren Myracle's style is so light and happy, it's impossible not to like one of her books in at least some way. <br/>I thought this book was really dramatic, thou</span>
  <span id="freeText13944988794989661061" style="display:none">**WARNING: includes spoilers**<br /><br />Another awesome novel by Lauren Myracle =)<br /> I wouldn't say this is her best, but it's still pretty good. I couldn't relate at all, though-- fifth grade was not like this for me. There were no ice cream socials, no Greek weeks, no Medusas, none of that. <br />Still, this is a very fun read. Finished it in one sitting. Lauren Myracle's style is so light and happy, it's impossible not to like one of her books in at least some way. <br />I thought this book was really dramatic, though, and could have done with less of:<br /><br />Milla- "sob sob I lost my turtle sob sob"<br />Modessa- "there there... *fake friendness*"<br />Violet- "Why didn't I just give it to her?? Stupid me!"<br />Katie-Rose's fantasies- "Oh, I wish life was PERFECT and Milla and I were BEST FRIENDS!"<br />Yasaman- "Oh, I wish I could have friends..."<br /><br />etc....<br /><br />So, yeah. I enjoyed the book, but it could have done well without so much of the above. I'd recommend it to my friends, though =)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13944988794989661061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_75201542" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/75201542" />
    <a title="Aprile" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1900151-aprile"><img alt="Aprile" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263603949p2/1900151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/75201542?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aprile" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aprile" href="/user/show/1900151-aprile">Aprile</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1900151-aprile?shelf=first-reads">first-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer75201542" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16809911483125542712">I won this book on goodreads, and although it is intended for a younger audience, I figured I'd read it for the fun of it. I thought the book was cute, with interesting characters and "poetic justice" for the mean girls.<br/><br/>However, I found that the suggested age level was a little young for the subject matter. Especially the computer stuff. Although it is a different time, I don't really agree with 10-11 year olds being on chatrooms and IM, so I don't think I would let a girl of that age read this </span>
  <span id="freeText16809911483125542712" style="display:none">I won this book on goodreads, and although it is intended for a younger audience, I figured I'd read it for the fun of it. I thought the book was cute, with interesting characters and "poetic justice" for the mean girls.<br /><br />However, I found that the suggested age level was a little young for the subject matter. Especially the computer stuff. Although it is a different time, I don't really agree with 10-11 year olds being on chatrooms and IM, so I don't think I would let a girl of that age read this book. Also, I thought the mean girls were horrible, a lot worse than girls I met at that age, and I don't agree with getting revenge on people, which I really think is a bad example for a young girl. I think overall the book would be better if the girls in the book were in grade 7 or 8 and the age catagory follow suit. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16809911483125542712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3443296005" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3443296005" />
    <a title="The Empress" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40870491-the-empress"><img alt="The Empress" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569463680p2/40870491.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3443296005?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="The Empress" class="user" itemprop="url" name="The Empress" href="/user/show/40870491-the-empress">The Empress</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40870491-the-empress?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3443296005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16176075325998437588">There are books that come into certain points of your life, especially when you need them, and change you forever. <i>This</i> was one of those books for me. <br/><br/>Picture a short, awkward, eleven-year-old Mexican girl who has just started sixth grade and is having trouble making friends. Her school librarian, someone she has come to see as a mother figure, recommends her a book series that <i>her</i> daughter has just started reading. After helping her find the book, the girl sits down and begins to read it, unawa</span>
  <span id="freeText16176075325998437588" style="display:none">There are books that come into certain points of your life, especially when you need them, and change you forever. <i>This</i> was one of those books for me. <br /><br />Picture a short, awkward, eleven-year-old Mexican girl who has just started sixth grade and is having trouble making friends. Her school librarian, someone she has come to see as a mother figure, recommends her a book series that <i>her</i> daughter has just started reading. After helping her find the book, the girl sits down and begins to read it, unaware that she will forever be changed by this book.<br /><br />The book itself is a relatively simple one: we follow four preteen girls (all named after flowers) as they begin their first week of fifth grade. A chance encounter sets in motion a series of events that, like reading this book did to me, will forever change these girls' lives. The friendship these "flowers" find with each other only blossoms over the course of the series, but that's not why this book is so important to me. <br /><br />It's not technically a spoiler, but <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[one of the girls has two moms <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, and to an eleven-year-old me who was just beginning to understand her attraction to girls, this was a gamechanger. Before, I'd never <i>really</i> seen a gay couple in any media I consumed; everyone and everything was about straight couples. Rereading this series as a fully-realized lesbian, it's easy to read Katie-Rose's interest in Camilla as a budding crush. In her chapters, she often goes on about how beautiful or kind she finds Camilla, and repeatedly wishes she could be close friends with her. In the first chapter, Katie-Rose longly sighs Camilla's name as she gazes up at the sky, dreaming of becoming her friend. While this all could be brushed off as just a friendly admiration, as I child I could relate to Katie-Rose's feelings for her friend, not realizing that <i>I</i> also had a crush on a girl in my class that I dismissed as "friendship". <br /><br />On top of the lesbian undertones, this is first and foremost a story about friendship and kindness. This book was key in teaching me what friendship, real and <i>true</i> friendship, was all about. It made me want to actively try to go out of my way to be kind to others, even if at times the girls are mean or talk bad about their classmates. Rereading it periodically as I got older would help me realize that I was also in a bad friendship like Camilla's with Modessa and Quin. <br /><br />Overall, this is a cutesy book about the highs and lows of being a preteen girl, and the importance of having solid friendships. While providing me with some of the first lesbian representation I'd ever seen, it also provided me with important life lessons about friendship and kindness that I still carry with me to this day. I can't wait to get started on the next book as I continue my biannual reread of the series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16176075325998437588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2029445927" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2029445927" />
    <a title="Jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58578943-jennifer"><img alt="Jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471012163p2/58578943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2029445927?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer" href="/user/show/58578943-jennifer">Jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810989825" href="/book/show/8740199-luv-ya-bunches">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58578943-jennifer?shelf=stuck-in-the-middle">stuck-in-the-middle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2029445927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16839162329223186183">I read this book to preview it for our school library. The main characters are entering 5th grade, so I assumed the book was aimed at girls of a similar age. <br/><br/>This book has plenty of mean girl action in it. The main characters strive to stay nice and act kindly despite the antics of the popular (mean) girls.<br/><br/>Overall I thought the book was appropriate, but there were a few things that bothered me:<br/><br/>1) In general, why do the mean girls always have to be the most popular kids? I know the hope is that a</span>
  <span id="freeText16839162329223186183" style="display:none">I read this book to preview it for our school library. The main characters are entering 5th grade, so I assumed the book was aimed at girls of a similar age. <br /><br />This book has plenty of mean girl action in it. The main characters strive to stay nice and act kindly despite the antics of the popular (mean) girls.<br /><br />Overall I thought the book was appropriate, but there were a few things that bothered me:<br /><br />1) In general, why do the mean girls always have to be the most popular kids? I know the hope is that a girl reading this book will relate to the nice girls and want to be more like them as they resolve their struggles without resorting to meanness. However, by always portraying the mean girls as the most popular, are we telling our kids that if that is what they desire then that is the way to do it? I feel if more books portrayed the mean girls as social climbers and not already on top, it would relate better as readers could see the characters at the time of when the choice of behavior is happening and before the outcome of popularity.<br /><br />2) I had no problem with one character having 2 moms, or the character with a mom in a mental institution. I DID have a problem with the author describing how one adult relative committed suicide by taking a whole bottle of Advil. Sorry, just not something I want to spell out step by step for my daughter. That whole scenario added nothing to the story other than a red flag.<br /><br />3) Bullying by exclusion. Both groups of girls do this. While I get the "if they don't want to play with you, play with the people that do" mentality, and I know including everyone is not always the answer either. Exclusion seems to be the main way characters treat each other in this book. I can offer no suggestions here, I just wish there was an alternative, as this is the type of bullying my 5th grade daughter sees most often in her life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16839162329223186183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2765674973" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2765674973" />

<div id="review_1284169184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <a title="Lilliana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lilliana" href="/user/show/33651376-lilliana">Lilliana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1284169184&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284169184" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7128654544718592821">Good Reads Review for Luv ya bunches<br/>By Lilliana Barnard<br/>	<br/>	Luv ya bunches starts out with a girl named Katie-rose. She had become close friends with one the most popular girls in fifth grade named Milla. They would talk about her (milla) old friends and how they were so rude. They had put dirt and mud in her milkshake! This is why Katie-Rose is so excited to go back to school. <br/>	There are 2 other important characters in the book.   <span id="freeText7128654544718592821" style="display:none">Good Reads Review for Luv ya bunches<br />By Lilliana Barnard<br />	<br />	Luv ya bunches starts out with a girl named Katie-rose. She had become close friends with one the most popular girls in fifth grade named Milla. They would talk about her (milla) old friends and how they were so rude. They had put dirt and mud in her milkshake! This is why Katie-Rose is so excited to go back to school. <br />	There are 2 other important characters in the book. Yasmin who is Muslim and Violet who is the new kid. All they want is for people to treat them right and to make some friends. <br />	So on the first day of school, Milla is walking down the hall and Yasmin accidently bumps into her. So now people call her spasmin. Millas bag opens up and everything falls out. She picks everything up, but then realizes she is missing tally. Tally is a very important bobble head turtle that is her “lucky” turtle. She starts to cry. Her “friend” Quin comes over to see what’s going on. When Milla tells her though, she acts like it’s the stupidest thing in the world and tells her it’s just some stupid bobble head toy. <br />	Later that day Milla was at recess with her friends Quin and Modessa (who Katie-rose likes to call Medusa). They decide to have the new girl, Violet, come over and hang out with them. The only thing was, violet doesn’t know if she even really wants to. But if she turns this opportunity down, she could become the most hated kid in school, so she decides to go hang out with them. <br />	Earlier in the day, Katie-roses class had been talking about the newest assignment coming up. What they were to do was dress up like greeks for their projects. So the next day, Katie-Rose wears a pair of sweatpants to school that say geek magnet. This is the day she wanted to go talk to Milla. So she figures that during recess would be a good time to talk to her. That didn’t work out so well though. Modessa, the mean one, as usual, had something to say about this. She decides to make fun of her pants. She was about to cry. Poor girl. <br />	During the end of the book, Katie-rose talked to Camilla (milla) about what had happened. They ended up being best friends in the end. They had a new group of four. It was Violet, Yasmin, Katie-Rose, and Camilla. They realized that they all have names that are also flowers. <br /><br />Main Characters: Modessa, Quinn, Camilla, Katie-rose, Violet, and Yasmin. <br /><br />Setting: This story took place in California over all. Smaller settings were the school, home, and the playground.<br /><br />Conflict: Camilla lost her lucky bobble-head turtle and is dying in the inside because of it. Soon Modessa finds it in between the couch cushions and the planted in Katie-Roses bag to make it look like she stole it. So now Camilla won’t talk to Katie-rose.<br /><br />Resolution of conflict: In the end, Violet talks to Camilla and tells her what happened. Camilla felt so bad for thinking Katie-rose would have taken it. They end up being best friends and the group of Modessa, Quinn, and Camilla… was gone.<br /><br />Opinion of the book: I enjoyed this book so much and anybody else would have too! I love how it talks in the four girls’ point of view. I feel that it was able to make the story a lot more interesting. I really was able to relate to any other typical fifth grader. <br /><br />Recommendation: I recommend this book to ages 4th grade and up. It has a few inappropriate words (1 or 2). If I was to rate this, I would give it away more than 5 stars.<br /></span>

<div id="review_385391650" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/385391650" />
    <a title="emilie.❤" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11588256-emilie"><img alt="emilie.❤" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520809364p2/11588256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/385391650?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="emilie.❤" class="user" itemprop="url" name="emilie.❤" href="/user/show/11588256-emilie">emilie.❤</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810989825" href="/book/show/8740199-luv-ya-bunches">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11588256-emilie?shelf=i-wish-i-could-unread-these">i-wish-i-could-unread-these</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11588256-emilie?shelf=mg">mg</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer385391650" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14313335666307932241">Ok, so I'll admit it, sometimes I like to read my little sister's books when I have nothing else to read and am just craving SOMETHING. I find some books for the 8-12 age group better written than YA. But this...was horrible on so many levels. I'm not sure where to begin.<br/><br/>Let me start with the unbelievably annoying characters. <br/>Katie-Rose: Why on God's green earth do people actually LIKE her?! I wanted to strangle the kid every time she opened her mouth. If I were to meet her in real life, she wou</span>
  <span id="freeText14313335666307932241" style="display:none">Ok, so I'll admit it, sometimes I like to read my little sister's books when I have nothing else to read and am just craving SOMETHING. I find some books for the 8-12 age group better written than YA. But this...was horrible on so many levels. I'm not sure where to begin.<br /><br />Let me start with the unbelievably annoying characters. <br />Katie-Rose: Why on God's green earth do people actually LIKE her?! I wanted to strangle the kid every time she opened her mouth. If I were to meet her in real life, she would definitely be that kind of kid you get annoyed just LOOKING at. There's just something about her that is so unspeakably irritating. <br />Milla was bland and boring, with the only memorable things about her being her mean-girl popular friends, and lesbian parents. <br />Violet was okay, I guess. A very forgettable character. In fact, the only thing I remember about her was that her mom was in a mental hospital, and that she took Milla's turtle for some reason. If I remember correctly, Lauren fails to explain Violet's actions in taking Tally the Turtle, so you're forced to go along with it and assume Violet "just wanted to." <br />And finally, we have Yasaman, the Muslim girl. I actually kind of liked her. She seemed smart and nice, and the least ditzy one out of all the girls. But HOW THE HECK DID SHE, A MEASLY LITTLE 10-YEAR-OLD, MANAGE TO MAKE A WEBSITE LIKE FACEBOOK? My brain could not comprehend that. Sure, she took a computer class and it would be believable if she made this website on Webs or something. But NO! She somehow makes this uber fancy website without ANY help, and no one even knows about it! Come on, no one's gonna buy that!<br /><br />The story is exactly like every other elementary or middle school book with a bunch of girls as the main characters. They all meet up somehow, battle the bullies, form a club, etc. The only thing slightly new and different about it was all the IM'ing, which I didn't like very much. When I was 10, I didn't sit around chatting or making websites. I made homemade magazines, played outside, made fake interview shows with my karaoke machine, tried my hand at baking cookies and did normal 10-year-old things. Something these girls <s>probably won't do anytime soon</s> will <i>never</i> do.<br /><br />She should also reaaaaaaally tone down on the exclamation points and question marks. Pretty much everything Katie-Rose (and a few other people) said was, "OH. EM. GEE!!! I LOVE YOUR BOOTS!!!!! WHERE DID YOU GET THEM?!?!" <br />Not those exact words, but the punctuation is pretty much the same. How does stuff like that get past her editor? Just...how? -.-<br /><br />I haven't read all of Myracle's books, but the ones I have read included some joke pertaining to male anatomy. I was hoping that since this is a book for little girls, I wouldn't find it there. But guess what? I did! What is up with this woman? She's like a 7th or 8th grader who still thinks "penis" is a funny word. I bet when she writes her stories, she sits at her computer and giggles all day just thinking about it. And she's only one year younger than my mom! Doesn't anyone besides me find this kind of disturbing?<br /><br />If you read this whole thing, I have to applaud you. Needless to say, I do not recommend this book to anyone. Not now, not ever.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14313335666307932241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YgxgkoArAR9AmaGj98Qs8Kg5ljlJOzNMOgDIHPwe9upjQ9K3avdo1WZbuNigTPQzJSzc1mW9gPgNF9+scIrReQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Julie’s the shy one: observer and artist, accidental fie<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3591163485794210076\" style=\"display:none\">Lydia Goldblatt and Julie Graham-Chang are best friends with one goal: to crack the code of popularity. Lydia’s the bold one: aspiring theater star, stick-fighting enthusiast, human guinea pig. Julie’s the shy one: observer and artist, accidental field hockey star, faithful recorder. In this notebook they write down their observations and carry out experiments to try to determine what makes the popular girls tick. But somehow, when Lydia and Julie try to imitate the popular girls, their efforts don’t translate into instant popularity. Lydia ends up with a bald spot, their parents won’t stop yelling, and Julie finds herself the number-one crush of Roland Asbjørnsen. Worse, they seem to be drifting farther and farther from their goal—and each other.\nAmy Ignatow’s hilarious debut novel introduces the intrepid fifth-graders Julie and Lydia, whose quest to understand popularity may not succeed in the ways they want, but will succeed in keeping readers in stitches.\n  From Bulletin of the Center for Children\'s Books:\n\nLydia and Julie, BFFs since birth, are now preparing to enter junior high, and they\'re on a mission to become popular. First, however, they have to determine exactly how popularity is achieved, so they decide to approach the matter as any good scientist would: observe those creatures already at the height of popularity and apply said observations to themselves, in the hopes of cracking into that mysterious world of junior-high stardom. The two record their observations and the often spectacularly unsuccessful outcomes of their various social experiments in a scrapbook-like journal, complete with notes passed at school, lists of projected popularity goals, and credibly goofy and kidlike drawings. The story here is fairly familiar: the girls fail miserably at their first attempts at the A-list (Lydia\'s hair falls out after a botched dye job, among other disasters) but eventually find acceptance in the upper echelon, only to learn the valuable lesson that it\'s the people you\'re most comfortable around who make the best friends. The diary format, however, adds an extra dimension of funny, and as in Jeff Kinney\'s Wimpy Kid series about Greg Heffley, it allows Julie and Lydia to come alive through their witty dialogue, their perceptive commentary, and even their characteristic handwriting. Secondary characters shine as well, particularly Julie\'s embarrassing but ultimately charming two dads, along with Lydia\'s goth-punk sister, a font of random quips and junior high wisdom. The popular kids end up being far from perfect and each has issues of her own to contend with, making the actual friendships that form among the girls all the more endearing. Those waiting for the next installment of Greg Heffley\'s adventures will be well served by this amusing experiment in sixth-grade celebrity. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10893277150581153872">Lauren Myracle is the author of numerous young adult novels. She was born in 1969 in North Carolina. Lauren Myracle holds an MA in English from Colorado State University and an MFA in Writing for Children and Young Adults from Vermont College. she has written many novels, including the famous IM books, ttyl, ttfn, and l8r, g8r.<br/><br/>Her first novel, Kissing Kate, was selected as one of ALA's "Best Books</span>
  <span id="freeText10893277150581153872" style="display:none">Lauren Myracle is the author of numerous young adult novels. She was born in 1969 in North Carolina. Lauren Myracle holds an MA in English from Colorado State University and an MFA in Writing for Children and Young Adults from Vermont College. she has written many novels, including the famous IM books, ttyl, ttfn, and l8r, g8r.<br /><br />Her first novel, Kissing Kate, was selected as one of ALA's "Best Books for Young Adults" for the year 2004. It was named by Booklist as one of the "Top Ten Youth Romances" of the year, as well as one of the "Top Ten Books by New Writers." Her middle-grade novel, Eleven, came out 2004, followed by its YA sequels (Twelve, Thirteen, Thirteen Plus One) .</span>
